Wheat v. Brown


No. 04-015


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2004 MT 33; 320 Mont. 15; 85 P.3d 765; 2004 Mont. LEXIS 36


January 27, 2004, Submitted on Briefs  
February 18, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication March 4, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and For the County of Lewis and Clark, Cause No. BDV 2003-601, Honorable Jeffrey M. Sherlock, Presiding Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff senators brought a complaint against defendant Montana Secretary of State, arising out of the 2003 redistricting of the State of Montana into 100 house districts and 50 senate districts for election of the state legislature. The senators were assigned to redrawn districts. The First Judicial District Court, Lewis and Clark County, Montana, granted summary judgment for the senators. The Secretary of State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The senators were elected to office with terms to run for four years. The district assignments of the senators differed from the Districting and Apportionment Commission's assignment. The issue on appeal was whether the Montana Constitution prohibited the Montana Legislature from enacting legislation establishing a procedure for assigning holdover senators to newly-drawn districts? The appellate court concluded the Montana Constitution's mandate that the Commission effect redistricting was self-executing and that, as the history of implementation illustrated, the power to assign holdover senators to districts was an inherent part of the redistricting process. By granting redistricting authority to the Commission under the constitution, it denied the legislature any latitude to invoke its plenary powers. The legislation designed to transfer the power to assign holdover senators from the Commission to the legislature violated Mont. Const. art. V, § 14.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the district court's opinion.

Shoemaker v. Denke


No. 02-573


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant council member challenged a decision from the District Court of the Twentieth Judicial District, in and for the County of Sanders (Montana), which entered judgment in favor of respondent employee by dismissing the member's petition for judicial review from a final decision of the Montana Human Rights Commission (HRC).


OVERVIEW: The employee filed a sexual harassment claim against a mayor. As a result, the member began publishing articles about the employee. By his failure to meet the filing deadline established by the HRC, the member failed to prosecute his claim in the administrative forum.

In re S.H.


No. 03-370


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state requested termination of parental rights. The District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, In and For the County of Cascade, Montana, approved a substance abuse treatment for the mother. When the mother failed to comply, the district court decided that termination was in the children's best interests. The mother appealed.


OVERVIEW: Allegations were made that the children's stepfather was sexually abusing them. The children were removed from the home, and have been in foster care since. The district court did not err in allowing the guardian ad litem to present hearsay evidence. The mother failed to provide evidence that an error of law occurred or that a finding of fact was factually unsupported. The district court did not abuse its discretion in determining that the best interests of the children were served by terminating the mother's parental rights.

State v. Leeson


No. 02-764


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 354; 319 Mont. 1; 82 P.3d 16; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 816


May 29, 2003, Submitted on Briefs  
December 18, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication January 6, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, In and for the County of Cascade, Cause No. ADC-01-470-1, The Honorable Thomas M. McKittrick, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged in the District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, In and for the County of Cascade (Montana), with operation of an unlawful clandestine laboratory in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 45-9-132 (2001). Defendant filed a motion to dismiss, arguing that § 45-9-132 was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. The district court denied the motion. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that § 45-9-132 was unconstitutionally vague on its face because a person of ordinary intelligence could be in possession of supplies, equipment, and materials commonly used for purposes other than the manufacturing of methamphetamine, subjecting that person to arrest for operating an unlawful clandestine laboratory. The court stated that given the specificity of the relevant statutes, it was difficult to imagine how a person of ordinary intelligence could fail to understand what conduct was forbidden under § 45-9-132. Moreover, § 45-9-132 incorporated the requirement that a person have intent to operate an unlawful clandestine laboratory. It would be difficult, if not impossible, for a person to inadvertently purposely or knowingly take action in furtherance of the criminal production or manufacture of dangerous drugs. Defendant also contended that § 45-9-132 was unconstitutionally overbroad because it subjected innocent people to being arrested for possessing certain items that were otherwise legal to possess. The court stated that defendant had not demonstrated how § 45-9-132 might infringe on constitutionally protected rights in a real or substantial way.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's decision.

Great Falls Tribune v. Mont. PSC


No. 02-301


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 359; 319 Mont. 38; 82 P.3d 876; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 822; 32 Media L. Rep. 1531


January 16, 2003, Orally Argued and Submitted  
December 18, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected December 23, 2003. Released for Publication January 6, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, Cause No. CDV 2001-708, The Honorable Thomas C. Honzel, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: District court judgment reversed; case remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant media entities challenged a decision of the District Court of the First Judicial District, in and for the County of Lewis and Clark (Montana), which partially granted and partially denied the entities access to certain documents filed by respondent power company with respondent Montana Public Service Commission (PSC) under a protective order.


OVERVIEW: The company filed documents with the PSC under a protective order. The entities filed this action seeking the disclosure of certain information contained in the documents. The trial court partially granted the request. On appeal, the court reversed. The court first held that the intent of Mont. Const. art. II, §§ 9, 10 was to limit protection of privacy to natural human individuals. The court overruled Mt. States, Etc. v. Dept. of Pub. Serv. Reg., 194 Mont. 277 (1981), and its progeny to the extent the decisions relied on the Mont. Const. art. II, § 9 constitutional balancing test of the right of individual privacy against the public's right to examine documents or to observe the deliberations of all public bodies or agencies of state government and its subdivisions as a basis for protecting trade secrets and other confidential proprietary information of non-human entities. Because the media bypassed the PSC in filing this action, the court remanded the case to the PSC for its consideration. The court agreed that Mont. Admin. R. 38.2.5004, 38.5.5007(a) abrogated the PSC's duty to confirm a company's claims of confidentiality. The court ordered an amendment of the rules.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded.

 

355 of 699 DOCUMENTS


State v. Turbiville


No. 02-037


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 340; 318 Mont. 451; 81 P.3d 475; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 804


September 26, 2002, Submitted on Briefs  
December 15, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication December 31, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Sixteenth Judicial District, In and For the County of Fallon, Cause No. DC 2000-01, Honorable Joe L. Hegel, Presiding Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the District Court of the Sixteenth Judicial District, In and For the County of Fallon (Montana), of driving a motor vehicle while under the influence of alcohol. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the results of the preliminary breath test (PBT) and the later administered Intoxilyzer breath test should have been suppressed because the advisories given to him were inadequate and misleading, and thereby amounted to a violation of his due process rights. The supreme court disagreed. The PBT advisory did not provide misleading or inaccurate information. The advisory accomplished its intended purpose of informing defendant of his choices at the time of testing and of the potentially serious consequences of refusing to submit to a blood or breath test. It did not mislead defendant into forfeiting his right to a later hearing or otherwise disturb that right. If defendant would have refused the test and his license had been suspended, he would have nonetheless retained his right to contest the suspension in the district court. Thus, there was not a concomitant due process right of an opportunity to be heard safeguarded by the advisory. In addition, defendant's right to due process was not implicated by the advisory's failure to notify him of the right to judicial review in the event he refused the preliminary breath test.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

356 of 699 DOCUMENTS


State v. Tison


No. 03-244


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 342; 318 Mont. 465; 81 P.3d 471; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 817


August 28, 2003, Submitted on Briefs  
December 15, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication December 31, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Fourth Judicial District, In and For the County of Mineral, Cause No. DC 2000-31, Honorable John S. Henson, Presiding Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the District Court of the Fourth Judicial District, in and for the County of Mineral (Montana), of assault with a weapon, assault on a minor, and partner family member assault. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Due to defendant's bizarre conduct, his attorney requested that defendant receive a mental evaluation. Although a physician determined defendant to be competent, the trial court found him unfit to proceed and committed defendant under Mont. Code Ann. § 46-14-221. The trial court received an evaluation after 90 days that defendant was unfit to proceed and it was unknown when he would regain fitness. The trial court committed defendant for another 90 days, after which time defendant was found fit to proceed. Ultimately, he was found guilty. On appeal, the court reversed. The court rejected the State's claim that the trial court intended for defendant to receive treatment and further evaluation pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 46-14-202. After committing defendant, the trial court was required to review defendant's fitness within 90 days. At that time, defendant had not been found fit, nor was there a finding that it appeared that he would become fit in the foreseeable future. Absent such findings, the trial court was required to dismiss the criminal charges against defendant. After the 90-day period expired, the State lacked the power to proceed further with the criminal charges.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded.

State v. Adgerson

SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 284; 318 Mont. 22; 78 P.3d 850; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 737

Post-conviction relief denied at Adgerson v. State, 2006 Mont. Dist. LEXIS 389 (2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, Cause No. ADC 2001-195, The Honorable Dorothy McCarter, Judge presiding.
State v. Adgerson, 2002 Mont. Dist. LEXIS 1742 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After defendant's motion to dismiss the information was denied, the District Court of the First Judicial District, County of Lewis and Clark (Montana), convicted him of stalking, in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-220(1)(b), (3). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and the victim were divorced, and defendant repeatedly contacted the victim about issues not related to their children, in ways that frightened her for her safety. The victim was a probation officer who often worked with the judge and had an attorney/client relationship with the prosecutor in the case. Defense counsel failed to raise the issue of recusal or bias in the district court. Defendant's conviction was affirmed, as: (1) the issue of recusal was waived, in that it was not raised in the district court and the plain error doctrine did not apply; (2) ineffective assistance of counsel was not properly before the appellate court, as the record did not show an obvious impropriety, and there was no evidence as to why defense counsel failed to seek recusal; (3) Mont. OUTCOME: The district court's judgment was affirmed.

Farrier v. Teachers' Ret. Bd.


No. 02-561


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 278; 317 Mont. 509; 78 P.3d 1207; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 635


December 5, 2002, Submitted on Briefs  
October 7, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication October 22, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, Cause No. ADV 2001-402. The Honorable Dorothy McCarter, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner teacher retired from a school district and received retirement benefits from the Teachers' Retirement System (TRS). He later began teaching full-time at a university and contributed to the Optional Retirement Program (ORP). Appellant teachers' retirement board suspended his benefits. On review, the District Court of the First Judicial District of Lewis and Clark County, Montana, reversed the board's decision. The board appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the board contended the trial court erred in determining that the teacher could receive retirement benefits from the TRS while employed full-time by the state university and contributing to the ORP. The supreme court found that the trial court correctly found that the teacher was not a terminated member of the TRS, he was inactive, and Mont. Code Ann. § 19-20-804 only applied to terminated members. Thus, it did not apply to the teacher. The trial court also properly found that the teacher's service through his retirement from the school district, during which time the TRS accepted his contributions, counted as creditable service for the purposes of his TRS benefits. However, the trial court incorrectly interpreted the law when it held that the teacher was entitled to receive benefits while maintaining inactive status. The teacher was precluded from receiving any further TRS benefits until such time as his status with the TRS was that of a terminated member.


OUTCOME: The trial court's determination that the teacher could receive retirement benefits while employed full-time by the university was reversed, and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

 

359 of 699 DOCUMENTS


Griffin v. State


No. 02-014


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 267; 317 Mont. 457; 77 P.3d 545; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 445


January 9, 2003, Submitted on Briefs  
September 30, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication October 16, 2003.
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Griffin, 2004 Mont. Dist. LEXIS 1743, 2004 ML 2950 (2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Fourth Judicial District, In and for the County of Missoula, Cause No. D.V.-01-458. The Honorable Edward P. McLean, Judge presiding.
Griffin v. State, 312 Mont. 522, 55 P.3d 417, 2002 Mont. LEXIS 435 (Mont., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate challenged a decision of the District Court of the Fourth Judicial District, in and for the County of Missoula (Montana), which denied his petition filed against respondent State for post-conviction relief.


OVERVIEW: The inmate had pleaded guilty to 10 counts of sexual abuse of children. The trial court denied the inmate's petition for post-conviction relief. The court affirmed on appeal. While the inmate made numerous factual allegations relating to his claims, he neglected to provide affidavits, records, or other evidence to support his allegations. The court noted that allegations in a petition for post-conviction relief did not constitute evidence. The petition failed to meet the requirements of Mont. Code Ann. § 46-21-104(1)(c) (1999) and thus the trial court did not err in denying the petition. Although the inmate claimed that the trial court should have granted him an evidentiary hearing on the petition, such a hearing was not required and a trial court was permitted to dismiss the petition based solely on the files and records of the case pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 46-21-201(1)(a) (1999). Another case, which held that a statute prohibiting virtual child pornography unconstitutional, did not apply in this case. Neither Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-625(1)(e) (1997), under which the inmate was convicted, nor the inmate's crimes, dealt with pornography that was virtual in nature.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

360 of 699 DOCUMENTS


State v. Egdorf


No. 01-895


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 264; 317 Mont. 436; 77 P.3d 517; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 441


June 20, 2002, Submitted on Briefs  
September 25, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication October 10, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Fourteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Musselshell, Cause No. DC-01-08. Honorable John C. McKeon, Judge Presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, an outfitter, was convicted in the justice court of misdemeanor offenses, including failure to report the unlawful taking of wildlife by a client, Mont. Code Ann. §§ 37-47-344, 37-47-404 (1999), and possession of unlawfully taken game, Mont. Code Ann. § 87-3-112. He appealed for a trial de novo to the District Court of the Fourteenth Judicial District, Montana, which denied his motion to dismiss. Defendant pled guilty and appealed.


OVERVIEW: The facts giving rise to the charges occurred nearly two years before the charges. Defendant argued that the extended statute of limitations for commencing prosecution of misdemeanor fish and game offenses usurped his rights to Equal Protection and Due Process and that mandatory forfeiture of licenses was unconstitutional. The court disagreed. With regard to Equal Protection under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Mont. Const. art. II, § 4, the fish and game offenses at issue were, by legislative classification, not similarly situated with all other misdemeanors. Therefore, defendant failed to make the necessary threshold showing that the offenses were part of a similarly situated class that had subjected him to unequal treatment. With regard to Substantive Due Process, the State had a legitimate governmental interest in preserving its wildlife. Extending the period for prosecuting misdemeanor violations of fish and game laws to three years was reasonably related to that interest, as the offenses were not always readily discoverable, perpetrators often lived out-of-state, and undercover operations could span the course of several hunting seasons.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the district court.

 

361 of 699 DOCUMENTS


LaFournaise v. Mont. Developmental Ctr.


No. 02-776


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 240; 317 Mont. 283; 77 P.3d 202; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 419; 20 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 676; 149 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P59,781


May 29, 2003, Submitted on Briefs  
September 11, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication September 26, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Fifth Judicial District, In and for the County of Jefferson, Cause No. DV-2001-9922, Honorable Loren Tucker, Judge Presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee sued defendant employer for wrongful discharge. The employer moved for summary judgment, arguing the employee was covered by a collective bargaining agreement (CBA), and was precluded from relief under the Wrongful Discharge From Employment Act, Mont. Code Ann. §§ 39-2-901 to 915. The District Court of the Fifth Judicial District, In and for the County of Jefferson, Montana, granted summary judgment. The employee appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employee had in fact gone through a grievance process with the union, after which her union representative told her the union would not proceed further. The employee claimed that the collective bargaining agreement was a contract of adhesion, and defied public policy because it cut off any remedy. However, it was undisputed that the CBA covered disputes between the parties, and the right to bargain collectively was recognized under Montana law. Further, no standardized form of agreement existed in the instant case, and neither contracting party had dictated the terms of the CBA to the other. Consequently, theirs was not a contract of adhesion. Secondly, the Wrongful Discharge From Employment Act (WDEA), Mont. Code Ann. §§ 39-2-901 to 915, did not violate the employee's constitutional rights to full legal redress, and a jury trial. The WDEA did not apply to the discharge of an employee covered by a CBA pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 39-2-912(2), and the employee failed to put forth any argument to overcome the presumption that § 39-2-912(2) was constitutional. Finally, the employee was not left without a remedy or access to the courts. An action against her union was possible.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

362 of 699 DOCUMENTS


State v. Spotted Eagle


No. 02-452


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 172; 316 Mont. 370; 71 P.3d 1239; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 257


April 11, 2003, Heard and Submitted  
June 23, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication July 9, 2003.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Spotted Eagle v. Montana, 157 L. Ed. 2d 417, 124 S. Ct. 544, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 8280 (U.S., Nov. 10, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Ninth Judicial District, In and for the County of Pondera, Cause No. DC 2001-16. The Honorable Marc G. Buyske, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: District court order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to operating a motor vehicle under the influence of alcohol (DUI), a felony, in the District Court of the Ninth Judicial District, Pondera County, Montana. Defendant reserved the right to appeal the trial court's denial of his motion to dismiss, in which he argued that his four prior uncounseled tribal court DUI convictions could not be used to enhance his sentence. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to Scott v. Illinois, the Sixth Amendment right to counsel applied to state proceedings pursuant to the Fourteenth Amendment. An uncounseled conviction that resulted in "imprisonment," violated the Sixth Amendment. While Native Americans were citizens, the United States Constitution did not apply to Indian tribes. The Indian Civil Rights Act (ICRA), afforded many protections of the Bill of Rights to Native Americans, but the Sixth Amendment right to counsel was not included. Without much explanation, the Tenth Circuit, in United States v. Benally, held that, in sentencing an Indian, a court could consider the defendant's uncounseled tribal convictions. The Montana Supreme Court agreed, but focused on the issue of a tribe's sovereignty. In other words, treating tribal court judgments with the same deference as those of foreign sovereigns as a matter of comity. The ICRA enabled the tribes to adopt their own tribal court and criminal justice system. An uncounseled tribal conviction did not violate the Blackfeet tribal code and was valid in the tribal court. Thus, defendant's tribal convictions were valid in their inception and could be used to enhance his sentence.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

363 of 699 DOCUMENTS


Udelhoven v. Mont. Dep't of Pub. HHS (In re McGurran)


No. 02-768


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 145; 316 Mont. 188; 70 P.3d 734; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 220


March 20, 2003, Submitted on Briefs  
May 20, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication June 5, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, Cause No. ADR 2002-352, The Honorable Dorothy McCarter, Judge presiding.
In re Support Obligation of McGurran, 2002 MT 144, 310 Mont. 268, 49 P.3d 626, 2002 Mont. LEXIS 248 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for further proceedings consistent with this Opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The First Judicial District Court, Lewis and Clark County, Montana, dismissed the appellant mother's petition for judicial review of the denial of her request for arbitration of the child support obligation that was determined to be owed by respondent father for lack of subject matter jurisdiction. The mother appealed.


OVERVIEW: At issue on appeal was whether the mother's due process rights were violated when the district court dismissed her petition because she failed to comply with the service requirements of Mont. Code Ann. § 40-5-253(4). The father claimed on appeal that the mother failed to exhaust her administrative remedies. Nevertheless, he failed to cross-appeal the trial court's rejection of the argument; therefore, the argument was waived. Finally, as to the father's statement that a jurisdictional argument could be raised at any time, untimeliness of the request for arbitration would not be jurisdictional for purposes of seeking judicial review in the district court. The mother's original mailing of a copy of her petition to the father's attorney, which had not been challenged as untimely, was sufficient service upon the father. Dismissal of the petition for judicial review on the basis of failure to follow the Mont. Code Ann. §40-5-253(4) service requirements would violate the mother's right to due process of law. Therefore, the district court erred in dismissing the petition for judicial review.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the district court's judgment.

Wright v. Mahoney


No. 02-575


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 141; 316 Mont. 173; 71 P.3d 1195; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 218


February 27, 2003, Submitted on Briefs  
May 19, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication July 3, 2003.
Rehearing denied by Wright v. Mahoney, 2003 Mont. LEXIS 270 (Mont., July 3, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, In and For the County of Yellowstone, Cause No. DV 2002-586, Honorable G. Todd Baugh, Presiding Judge.
State v. Wright, 2001 MT 247, 307 Mont. 100, 38 P.3d 772, 2001 Mont. LEXIS 507 (2001)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Thirteenth Judicial District, County of Yellowstone, Montana, denied petitioner inmate's motion for writ of prohibition in order to prevent respondent Montana Department of Corrections from transferring the inmate from the Montana State Prison in Deer Lodge, Montana, to another correctional facility. The inmate appealed.


OVERVIEW: The inmate argued that the department of corrections did not have the authority to transfer him. Specifically, he argued under Mont. Code Ann. § 53-30-101 (1995), Deer Lodge was designated the state prison and any application of the amended statute, to include other institutions, was an unconstitutional ex post facto law. The appellate court noted that while the Fourteenth Amendment prohibited a state from depriving a person of life, liberty, or property without due process of law, it did not require the State to have more than one prison or guarantee that a prisoner would be placed in any particular prison if it had more than one prison. The appellate court held being transferred from one prison to another was not a significant hardship in relation to the ordinary incidents of prison life or that it had an adverse affect on the confinement. The appellate court concluded the inmate's sentence was to ensure that he served at least 20 years, not to confer on him a privilege to serve it in a particular prison.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

In re K.C.H.


No. 02-129


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 125; 316 Mont. 13; 68 P.3d 788; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 202


August 8, 2002, Submitted on Briefs  
April 29, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication May 15, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, In and For the County of Yellowstone, Cause No. DN-2000-055. Honorable Diane G. Barz, Presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father appealed from the judgment of the District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, Yellowstone County (Montana), which terminated his parental rights.


OVERVIEW: The father argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred by terminating his parental rights, as neither of his treatment plans were appropriate. The supreme court disagreed. While the father was correct that his treatment plans did not identify the problems or conditions that resulted in the abuse, as no actual abuse occurred, both of his plans did identify the problems or conditions creating the substantial risk of harm to the health and welfare of the child. Those were the lack of a stable home and safe environment. Because of the natural mother's demonstrated inability to care for her children, the father's treatment plans put the onus on him to provide a safe environment for the child. Both of the father's treatment plans listed one of his primary goals as "to provide a stable home for his child with adequate housing and income." Therefore, because the father's treatment plans did identify the threshold problems or conditions creating the substantial risk of harm to the health and welfare of the child, the plans were appropriate.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

366 of 699 DOCUMENTS


State v. Wagner


Nos. 01-879 and 01-880


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 120; 315 Mont. 498; 68 P.3d 840; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 208


October 10, 2002, Submitted on Briefs  
April 29, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication May 15, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Broadwater, Cause Nos. DV-01-16 and DC-01-26. The Honorable Jeffrey Sherlock, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After his motion to dismiss was denied, defendant pleaded guilty in the District Court of the First Judicial District, in and for the County of Broadwater (Montana), to driving under the influence. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred by denying his motion to dismiss because a police officer's encounter with defendant constituted an investigative stop for which he did not have particularized suspicion. The supreme court disagreed. The officer did not initiate a stop of defendant's vehicle. Instead, defendant exited the interstate, parked at a gas station, and entered the building on his own volition. The officer, after receiving a citizen tip regarding a possible drunk driver, subsequently requested that defendant accompany him outside for further questioning. There was no evidence in the record which indicated that the officers restrained defendant's liberty by means of physical force or show of authority. To the contrary, the evidence suggested that defendant was free to disregard the officer's request and walk away at any time during the initial encounter. In view of all of the circumstances surrounding the incident, the officer's initial actions did not amount to such a show of authority that a reasonable person would have believed he or she was not free to leave. Therefore, the encounter did not constitute an investigative stop.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Harris v. Smartt


No. 01-464


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 135; 316 Mont. 130; 68 P.3d 889; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 198



April 29, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication May 15, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: Harris v. Smartt, 2002 MT 239, 311 Mont. 507, 57 P.3d 58, 2002 Mont. LEXIS 501 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Montana Supreme Court previously found respondent judge had accessed sexually explicit material on his county-owned computer and suspended him from office. The supreme court further ordered him to pay all costs of the proceedings, approximately $ 50,000. The judge appealed the imposition of costs.


OVERVIEW: The judge argued the Montana Judicial Standards Commission was a constitutionally-mandated body under Mont. Code art. VII, § 11 and that the commission's authority was limited by the constitutional mandate. Specifically, he contended the state constitution did not explicitly or impliedly grant the commission any authority to adopt rules which assess costs and fees on a judge found to have violated the Montana Canons of Judicial Ethics. The supreme court initially noted the Montana Constitution was very explicit as to what sanctions it could impose on a justice or judge; namely, retirement, censure, suspension, or removal, and that the list of sanctions was exclusive. The supreme court held the express mention of the above sanctions implied the exclusion of non-expressed sanctions; therefore, the imposition of costs and attorney fees exceeded the power granted to either the commission or the supreme court by the Montana Constitution.


OUTCOME: The supreme court vacated that portion of its previous order imposing costs of the disciplinary proceeding on the judge.

Walker v. State


No. 01-528


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 134; 316 Mont. 103; 68 P.3d 872; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 206


September 5, 2002, Submitted on Briefs  
April 29, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication May 14, 2003. As Corrected May 6, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, In and for the County of Cascade, Cause No. CDC 94-337 The Honorable Kenneth R. Neill, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty in the District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, in and for the County of Cascade (Montana), to negligent arson and felony forgery. Defendant filed a petition for postconviction relief, which was denied. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred by denying his motion for postconviction relief because the Montana State Prison (MSP) practices regarding behavior management plans (BMP) violated defendant's right to be free from cruel and unusual punishment. The supreme court agreed. Numerous inmates who resided in A-block testified about the filthy, uninhabitable cells. Those inmates testified that the cells commonly had blood, feces, vomit and other types of debris in the cells they were forced to inhabit. One inmate recounted an instance where he was placed in a cell with human waste rubbed all over the walls and vomit in the corner. Reading Mont. art. II, §§ 4 and 22 together, the BMPs and the living conditions on A-block constituted an affront to the inviolable right of human dignity possessed by defendant and such punishment constituted cruel and unusual punishment when it exacerbated defendant's mental health condition. The Montana Constitution forbid correctional practices which permitted prisons in the name of behavior modification to disregard the innate dignity of human beings, especially in the context where those persons suffered from serious mental illness.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded to the district court for entry of an order requiring MSP to conform the operations of its administrative segregation units to the requirements of the opinion and to report, in writing to the district court within 180 days as to the actions taken.

 

369 of 699 DOCUMENTS


State v. Mathis


No. 01-671


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 112; 315 Mont. 378; 68 P.3d 756; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 187


October 17, 2002, Submitted on Briefs  
April 25, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication May 14, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Fourth Judicial District, In and for the County of Missoula, Cause No. DC-01-98, The Honorable Douglas G. Harkin, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the District Court of the Fourth Judicial District, in and for the county of Missoula (Montana), which convicted him of speeding in a construction zone.


OVERVIEW: The Montana Department of Transportation (DOT) contracted with a company to perform construction on an interstate. A subcontractor was hired to post speed limit signs. Defendant was issued a speeding ticket while traveling through the construction zone. After defendant was convicted, he sought review in the district court. The district court upheld the conviction, and defendant sought review. In affirming, the court determined that Mont. Code § 61-8-314 was a constitutional delegation of legislative authority to an administrative agency or private party. The implicit rationale behind § 61-8-314 was to establish speed limits in construction zones based on traffic conditions or the condition of the project. Enumerating every possible factor for consideration would have been an impossible task for the legislature. Therefore, it was reasonable to delegate the duty to the entities most familiar with the nature of each project. Moreover, the district court did not err in denying defendant's motion to dismiss based on insufficient evidence. Section 61-8-314 did not grant the authority to set speed limits exclusively to the DOT, and the parties sufficiently complied with § 61-8-314(4)(ii).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction.

Hardy v. Progressive Specialty Ins. Co.


No. 02-448


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 85; 315 Mont. 107; 67 P.3d 892; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 161


January 23, 2003, Argued; January 30, 2003, Submitted  
April 18, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication May 5, 2003.
As Corrected June, 10, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: ORIGINAL PROCEEDING: Certified Question, United States District Court. District of Montana, Missoula Division. The Hon. Donald W. Molloy, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Certified questions were answered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff insured filed an action against defendant insurer to recover damages. The United States District Court, District of Montana, Missoula Division, certified three questions of law: (1) was the offset provision void; (2) was it against public policy to charge separate premiums for coverage, if the insured could only collect one amount of coverage; and (3) were policies void if they included certain provisions that defeated coverage.


OVERVIEW: The insured was injured in an accident. He sought compensation under the underinsured motorist (UIM) coverage he had for three of his vehicles. A separate premium was paid for each of the three UIM coverages. The court agreed with the insured that the policy in question was ambiguous. The court concluded that the UIM definition and offset clause in the policy violated the insured's reasonable expectations. Thus, the court held that the offset provision and definition of underinsured motorist in the policy violated state public policy, as they created an ambiguity regarding coverage and rendered coverage that the insured promised to provide illusory. The court further held that Mont. Code Ann. § 33-23-203 was not rationally related to the stated objective of maintaining affordable insurance in the state and constituted arbitrary and capricious action. Thus, the statute, to the extent that it allowed charging premiums for illusory coverage, was unconstitutional. The anti-stacking provision in the policy violated public policy. A policy that contained provisions that defeated coverage for which the insurer received valuable consideration was also against public policy.


OUTCOME: The court held that: (1) the offset provision violated public policy; (2) the anti-stacking provision was against public policy and a statute, to the extent that it allowed charging premiums for illusory coverage, was unconstitutional; and (3) policies, under which an insurer received consideration for coverage, that contained provisions that defeated that coverage were against public policy.

Bradley v. Crow Tribe of Indians


No. 02-474


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 82; 315 Mont. 75; 67 P.3d 306; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 158


February 6, 2003, Submitted on Briefs  
April 15, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication April 30, 2003.
As Corrected June, 10, 2003.
Appeal after remand at, Remanded by Bradley v. Crow Tribe of Indians, 2005 MT 309, 2005 Mont. LEXIS 504 (Dec. 9, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Yellowstone, Cause No. DV 00-0980, The Honorable Susan P. Watters, Judge presiding.
Bradley v. Crow Tribe of Indians, 2002 Mont. Dist. LEXIS 2745 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff consultant challenged a decision from the District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, Yellowstone County (Montana), which granted defendant tribe's motion to dismiss a breach of contract action.


OVERVIEW: The consultant entered into a contract with the tribe. He agreed to act as a director for the planning and construction of a power plant on tribal property. Because the person who negotiated the contract was later removed from office, the agreement was terminated. However, the consultant already provided services to the tribe. The consultant filed a breach of contract action; however, a signed copy of the agreement was never located. The district court entered judgment in favor of the consultant, but later granted the tribe's motion to dismiss. In reversing, the court determined that the district court erred in finding that the tribe had not unequivocally waived its immunity from suit in state court. The district court erred in concluding that an equitable exception to the Montana Statute of Frauds, Mont. Code Ann. § 28-2-903(1), was unable to overcome sovereign immunity. The district court should not have considered § 28-2-903(1) in determining whether it had subject matter jurisdiction over the case. Whether the waiver was unequivocal depended on the language of the contract. The consultant's complaint alleged sufficient facts to vest the district court with jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision to dismiss the consultant's complaint. The case was remanded to the district court for further proceedings.

Schmill v. Liberty Northwest Ins. Corp.


No. 01-663


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 80; 315 Mont. 51; 67 P.3d 290; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 153


February 13, 2003, Submitted on Briefs  
April 10, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication April 25, 2003.
Judgment entered by, Costs and fees proceeding at Schmill v. Liberty Northwest Ins. Corp., 2005 MT 144, 2005 Mont. LEXIS 226 (June 7, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: Workers' Compensation Court, State of Montana. The Honorable Mike McCarter, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner claimant filed a petition in the Montana Workers' Compensation Court, alleging that she suffered from an occupational disease arising from her employment. The employer was insured by respondent insurer. The workers' compensation court held that Mont. Code Ann. §39-72-706 was unconstitutional and awarded claimant full benefits without reduction for non-occupational factors. The insurer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The claimant began experiencing heel pain after working as a waitress for five years and was given a three-percent physical impairment rating of the whole body. The claimant requested an impairment award for disability caused by her occupational disease pursuant to the Workers' Compensation Act (WCA). The claimant contended that, because Mont. Code Ann. §39-72-706 of the Occupational Disease Act (ODA) required reduction of disability benefits for non-occupational factors, while the WCA did not, her right to equal protection was violated. The court agreed that the ODA and the WCA treated similarly situated classes of workers differently. Furthermore, apportioning the claimant's permanent impairment award for her occupational disease pursuant to § 39-72-706 of the ODA while providing full benefits for injured workers pursuant to the WCA was not rationally related to a legitimate governmental interest. The court thus concluded that §39-72-706 violated the equal protection guarantee found at Mont. Const. art. II, § 4.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the workers' compensation court.

State v. Hamilton


No. 02-305


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 71; 314 Mont. 507; 67 P.3d 871; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 142


October 31, 2002, Submitted on Briefs  
April 4, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication April 24, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Gallatin, Cause No. DC 01-223. The Honorable Mark Guenther, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the District Court of the Eighteenth Judicial District, Gallatin County (Montana), of criminal possession of dangerous drugs. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred by denying her motion to suppress evidence, as she had an objectively reasonable expectation of privacy in her lost wallet, and the police conducted an illegal inventory search of the wallet. The supreme court agreed. Defendant had an objectively reasonable expectation of privacy in her lost wallet. Her expectation of privacy was diminished only to the extent necessary for the police to determine ownership. Defendant testified that a driver's license was displayed in the right compartment and a checkbook was displayed in the left compartment, both of which would have been apparent upon releasing the flap and opening the wallet. While the officer could not recall if he saw the checkbook or driver's license before he conducted his search, he admitted it was possible that the identification information was readily apparent. Finally, when another officer took custody of the wallet, he found a driver's license, a social security card, and a checkbook that all indicated defendant owned the lost wallet. It was clear that there was no need to search the coin purse to determine that defendant owned the lost wallet.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded.

State v. Martinez


Nos. 00-781 & 00-802


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 65; 314 Mont. 434; 67 P.3d 207; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 68


October 2, 2001, Heard; December 12, 2002, Submitted  
April 1, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication April 17, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Yellowstone, Cause Nos. DC 99-0859 & DC 99-863. The Honorable Russell C. Fagg, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for further proceedings consistent with this Opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants pleaded guilty in the District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, Yellowstone County (Montana), to drug offenses. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendants argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred by denying the motion to suppress evidence, as the police lacked particularized suspicion for the stop of the vehicle, as the confidential informant's (CI) tip was not reliable. The supreme court agreed. The CI had not established a track record that supported a finding of reliability. The record did not support the trial court's finding that the CI personally observed the criminal behavior that she reported to the police. No officer involved in the investigation testified that the CI listened in on discussions of defendants' plans for the illegal activities. In fact, there was no evidence presented at the suppression hearing indicating that the officers even asked the CI how she came by her incriminating information. The record revealed nothing about the source of the CI's knowledge. Therefore, the trial court erred by finding the CI's tip to be reliable. Consequently, the tip did not stand as an independent basis for the investigative stop. Therefore, the trial court erred by denying the motion to suppress.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded.

 

375 of 699 DOCUMENTS


State v. Davison


No. 02-581


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 64; 314 Mont. 427; 67 P.3d 203; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 71


January 16, 2003, Submitted on Briefs  
April 1, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication April 17, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, In and For the County of Cascade, Cause No. ADC-2001-460, Honorable Thomas M. McKittrick, Presiding Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with two counts of operating an unlawful clandestine laboratory, Mont. Code Ann. § 45-9-132, and one count of criminal possession of dangerous drugs, Mont. Code Ann. § 45-9-102. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the first charge. Defendant appealed the judgment of the Eighth Judicial District Court, Cascade County (Montana), which denied his motion to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant argued that Mont. Code Ann. § 45-9-132, which proscribed the operation of unlawful clandestine laboratories, was unconstitutional. Specifically, defendant argued that the statute violated the Equal Protection Clauses of the federal and Montana Constitutions, as it provided for a greater penalty than Mont. Code Ann. § 45-9-110, which proscribed the manufacture of dangerous drugs. The state supreme court concluded that the offense of operating an unlawful clandestine laboratory and the offense of manufacturing dangerous drugs were separate and distinct crimes and that an individual convicted under one statute was not, for purposes of the equal protection guarantee, similarly situated to an individual convicted under the other statute. The aim of Mont. Code Ann. § 45-9-110 was to deter the production of narcotics and to eliminate the detrimental effect of dangerous drugs generally. However, Mont. Code Ann. § 45-9-132 was enacted with the purpose of criminalizing the hazards posed by the use of methamphetamine labs. Defendant had not made the requisite showing that Mont. Code Ann. § 45-9-132 affected similarly situated classes in an unequal manner.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the district court's denial of defendant's motion to dismiss.

 

376 of 699 DOCUMENTS


Stavenjord v. Mont. State Fund


No. 01-630


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 67; 314 Mont. 466; 67 P.3d 229; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 69


March 28, 2002, Argued; April 18, 2002, Submitted  
April 1, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication April 17, 2003.
Subsequent appeal at, Remanded by Stavenjord v. Mont. State Fund, 2006 MT 257, 2006 Mont. LEXIS 489 (Oct. 6, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: Workers' Compensation Court, State of Montana, The Honorable Mike McCarter, Judge Presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee argued that she was denied equal protection, as the benefits provided to her for partial disability pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 39-72-405(2) (1997) were less than provided for the same partial disability pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 39-71-703 (1997). Respondent, the Montana State Fund, appealed the judgment of the Workers' Compensation Court (Montana), which concluded that the employee was denied equal protection.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the issues were: (1) whether the trial court abused its discretion when it declined to reopen the record at the request of the Fund to allow additional evidence of the historical and anatomical differences between injuries and occupational diseases; and (2) whether the trial court erred when it concluded that Mont. Code Ann. § 39-72-405 (1997) of the Occupational Disease Act violated the Equal Protection Clause of the Montana Constitution. Because there was no indication that the evidence that the Fund sought to offer following trial was not available prior to trial and because the evidence of other claims for occupational disease were not material to the issue involved in the case, the state supreme court concluded that the trial court did not abuse its discretion when it denied the Fund's motion to reopen the evidence. In addition, the state supreme court concluded that, providing partial disability benefits to a person in the employee's situation in the amount of $ 27,027 under the Workers' Compensation Act, but limiting her wage supplement to $ 10,000 under the Occupational Disease Act violated the Equal Protection Clause found at Mont. Const. art. II, § 4.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Finke v. State ex rel. McGrath


No. 01-815


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 48; 314 Mont. 314; 65 P.3d 576; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 50


May 30, 2002, Heard; June 6, 2002, Submitted  
March 18, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected March 24, 2003. Released for Publication April 2, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: In the Supreme Court of the State of Montana. Original Proceeding--Declaratory and Injunctive Relief. In and for the County of Yellowstone.
Finke v. State, 308 Mont. 390, 40 P.3d 999, 2001 Mont. LEXIS 688 (Mont., 2001)



DISPOSITION: A Senate Bill is stricken.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff individuals and plaintiff municipalities sought a declaratory judgment, in the original proceeding brought against defendant state, defendant department of labor and industry, and defendant counties, on the constitutionality of SB 242, 2001 Leg. Sess. (Mont. 2001).


OVERVIEW: The individuals did not own real property in their municipal jurisdictional areas (MJAs), but all qualified as eligible voters. The municipalities previously sought and obtained authorization to create MJAs and to exercise building code jurisdiction over their respective donut areas. The election provisions of SB 242 were challenged on several constitutional grounds: as violations of Mont. Const. art. II, §§ 4, 13 and 17 and Mont. Const. art. V, § 11(3), and as violations of the Fifth and Fourteenth Amendments of the U.S. Constitution. In a similar vein, the State argued that a franchise limitation such as that found in SB 242 did not necessarily violate equal protection principles. The appellate court disagreed. A law restricting the franchise could be upheld only upon the State showing a compelling interest. The supreme court concluded that the election provisions of SB 242 disenfranchised constituents who were not record owners of real property, in violation of the Montana and the U.S. Constitutions. Moreover, the supreme court concluded that the unconstitutional provisions of the law were not severable. Finally, the request for attorney fees was denied.


OUTCOME: The supreme court struck the law in its entirety.

Miller v. City of Red Lodge


No. 01-517


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 44; 314 Mont. 278; 65 P.3d 562; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 46


April 18, 2002, Submitted on Briefs  
March 13, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication March 28, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Twenty-Second Judicial District, In and for the County of Carbon, Cause No. DV 00-37, The Honorable Russell C. Fagg, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for further proceedings consistent with this Opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner brought suit against defendant dog owner. The dog owner brought a third-party complaint against third-party defendant city, alleging violation of due process, equal protection, statutory infirmity, double jeopardy claims, and a claim arising out of 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. Upon motion by the city, the 22nd Judicial District, Carbon County, Montana, granted summary judgment to the city. The dog owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The dog owner lived in a rental property that belonged to the property owner. The property owner visited the dog owner's residence to perform an inspection on the property. The dog owner's dog bit the property owner, causing injury. The city court judge ordered that the dog owner post a bond, that the police department seize the dog, and board the dog at the dog owner's expense. The dog owner could not afford the bond, so she had the dog euthanized. The sole issue on appeal was whether the district court erred when it granted the city's motion for summary judgment on the § 1983 claim pursuant to the doctrines of judicial and quasi-judicial immunity. The appellate court overruled previous case law, to the extent that it afforded the local government units immunity from § 1983 liability pursuant to the doctrines of judicial and quasi-judicial immunity. The district court applied the previous case law, which misconstrued and misapplied § 1983 immunities.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court's order, and the matter was remanded.

 

379 of 699 DOCUMENTS


Mont. Media, Inc. v. Flathead County


No. 01-694


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2003 MT 23; 314 Mont. 121; 63 P.3d 1129; 2003 Mont. LEXIS 23


August 8, 2002, Submitted on Briefs  
February 13, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication March 3, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eleventh Judicial District, In and for the County of Flathead, Cause No. DV-00-267B, The Honorable Stewart E. Stadler, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: District court judgment and injunction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The District Court of the Eleventh Judicial District, in and for the County of Flathead (Montana), denied appellant billboard company's petition for declaratory relief against respondent city and permanently enjoined it from operating its signs that violated the city ordinance. The trial court denied its petition for declaratory relief against respondent county in part and postponed action on its equal protection claim. The company appealed.


OVERVIEW: The company offered billboard space for business advertisements. The billboards were subject to regulation pursuant to the city or the county's ordinances. After a road widening project forced the company to move a number of its billboards, the company's billboards were not brought into compliance with the ordinances. The ordinances directly advanced the city and county's goals of reducing visual blight and traffic hazards. Also, the ordinances reached no further than necessary to accomplish their objectives. Thus, the ordinances did not impose an unconstitutional restriction on commercial speech. Additionally, because the city's and county's officials did not have unfettered discretion regarding the issuance of permits, the ordinances did not create an unconstitutional prior restraint on speech. Further, the ordinances' procedural safeguards were sufficient and there was no violation of due process. Finally, the city did not violate the equal protection clause of either the state or federal constitutions when it treated the city's welcome sign, a non-commercial sign, differently than the company's commercial signs.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment and determined that the city did not violate the equal protection clause of either the state or federal constitutions.

 

380 of 699 DOCUMENTS


State v. McLeod


No. 01-140


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2002 MT 348; 313 Mont. 358; 61 P.3d 126; 2002 Mont. LEXIS 633


November 29, 2001, Submitted on Briefs  
December 30, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication January 15, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Yellowstone, The Honorable Russell Fagg, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to felony drug possession, violating Mont. Code Ann. § 45-9-102, and a misdemeanor violation, Mont. Code Ann. § 45-10-103. The Thirteenth Judicial District Court (Montana) sentenced him to the maximum five-year sentence and $ 1,000 fine and a concurrent six-month sentence. Defendant challenged the legality of the sentence.


OVERVIEW: The sentence was upheld although the district court relied on incorrect information in the pre-sentence investigation (PSI). Defendant extensively reviewed the PSI prior to the sentencing hearing and he and his counsel were afforded an opportunity to discuss the content of the PSI prior to the hearing. Neither objected to or challenged the content of the PSI that defendant later challenged on appeal. Under these circumstances, the court held that defendant's affirmation of the correctness of the information in the PSI constituted a waiver, under Orsborn, of his due process challenge. The district court provided the due process necessary to avert the utilization of misinformation in sentencing, and the sentence imposed did not exceed statutory limitations. However, as to defendant's other contention, the transcript did not show the court made a serious inquiry or separate determination, as required by Mont. Code Ann. § 46-18-231(3), into the defendant's ability to pay the fine nor inquiry into his financial resources or the nature of the burden that the $ 1,000 fine would impose. Thus the fine was improper.


OUTCOME: The conviction and sentence were affirmed, but the fine was reversed in part, and the case remanded.

State v. Shook


No. 99-608


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2002 MT 347; 313 Mont. 347; 67 P.3d 863; 2002 Mont. LEXIS 639


October 4, 2001, Submitted on Briefs  
December 30, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied February 11, 2003. Released for Publication February 11, 2003.
Amended by, Rehearing denied by State v. Shook, 2002 MT 347A, 2003 Mont. LEXIS 25 (Feb. 11, 2003)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Shook v. Montana, 157 L. Ed. 2d 30, 124 S. Ct. 67, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 5555 (U.S., 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Twentieth Judicial District, In and for the County of Sanders, The Honorable C. B. McNeil, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the District Court of the Twentieth Judicial District, County of Sanders (Montana), which upheld her conviction for hunting within a closed area, in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 87-1-304.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot and killed a whitetail buck on private property within the boundaries of an Indian reservation. Under regulations promulgated by the Montana Fish, Wildlife, and Parks Commission, big game hunting privileges were limited only to tribal members. She was charged with hunting in a closed area. After defendant pled guilty, she reserved her right to a review of the denial of her motion to dismiss the charge. The district court determined that the regulation did not violate the Montana Constitution. In affirming, the court determined that the regulation in question was not an unconstitutional violation of equal protection. Laws that afforded Indians special treatment were constitutional, as long as they could have been tied rationally to the fulfillment of the unique federal obligation toward Indians. Moreover, Indian treaties were regarded as part of Montana law. Therefore, Montana was permitted to regulate the hunting activities of non-tribal members, unless Congress or tribal self-governance matters precluded it. Despite the fact that the treaties in each tribe were unique, the Commission was permitted to apply a statewide rule.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction.

State v. Courville


No. 00-800


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2002 MT 330; 313 Mont. 218; 61 P.3d 749; 2002 Mont. LEXIS 621


September 20, 2001, Submitted on Briefs  
December 20, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication January 8, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Twentieth Judicial District, In and for the County of Lake, The Honorable C. B. McNeil, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the District Court of the Twentieth Judicial District, in and for the County of Lake (Montana), of felony assault on a police officer. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred by denying his motion to suppress evidence because the arresting officer did not have particularized suspicion of wrongdoing. The supreme court disagreed. Defendant was stopped after officers received a report of a drinking party, and that defendant's truck had left the party. As defendant was being arrested, he struck the officer in the face a number of times. Defendant jerked the officer's gun away from him and hit him in face with it. Defendant then got off of the officer, threw the gun into the back of his truck, and attempted to leave. The officer got to his feet and was able to retrieve the gun. He attempted to stop defendant but was unable to and defendant fled the scene. The evidence of defendant's criminal conduct committed against the officer was so attenuated from the claimed improper investigatory stop that the evidence of his conduct lost its primary constitutional taint--if, indeed there ever was any--and that evidence was, therefore, not subject to the exclusionary rule. Therefore, the trial court did not err by denying defendant's motion to suppress.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Reidelbach v. Burlington N. & Santa Fe Ry. Co.


No. 02-242


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2002 MT 289; 312 Mont. 498; 60 P.3d 418; 2002 Mont. LEXIS 563


September 5, 2002, Submitted on Briefs  
December 10, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication December 27, 2002.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, In and for the County of Cascade, The Honorable Julie Macek, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, an injured railroad employee, appealed the order of the District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, Cascade County, Montana, dismissing various counts of his complaint against respondents, the railway company and its claims representative, based upon the conclusion that the Federal Employers' Liability Act, 45 U.S.C.S. § 51 et seq., was the exclusive remedy available to the injured employee.


OVERVIEW: The employee worked for a railway company for 20 years. Over time, the employee incurred serious back injuries as a result of lifting loads of 200 to 300 pounds. Ultimately, he was forced to stop working. A claims representative working on behalf of the railway offered him an agreement under which the employee would forego pursuing his legal rights under the Federal Employers' Liability Act (FELA), 45 U.S.C.S. § 51 et seq., in return for a proposed settlement. However, no settlement offer was ever made. The employee filed suit under FELA for his physical injuries and under state law for post-injury claims mishandling. The railway raised federal preemption as a bar to the state law claims. The court reviewed the matter and disagreed. The state claims were not preempted by FELA, which did not provide a remedy for post-injury claims mishandling. Montana had an overriding interest in protecting its citizens from bad faith claims practices pursuant to the Uniform Trade Practices Act, Mont. Code Ann. § 33-18-101 et seq. The employee was permitted to proceed with his action.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the matter was remanded.

Kottel v. State


No. 01-833


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2002 MT 278; 312 Mont. 387; 60 P.3d 403; 2002 Mont. LEXIS 547


March 28, 2002, Submitted on Briefs  
December 5, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication December 23, 2002.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, In and for the County of Cascade, The Honorable Thomas M. McKittrick, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers appealed an order of the Eighth Judicial District Court, Cascade County (Montana), granting summary judgment to respondents, the State of Montana, the county treasurer, and the county commissioners (hereinafter State), and holding that the tax levy for the vocational technical schools (vo-techs) pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 20-25-439(1) did not violate the state or federal constitutions.


OVERVIEW: The vo-techs were partially funded by the property taxes in the counties in which they were located. The supreme court held that the district court erred when it determined that the protections of the uniformity rule were no longer in Mont. Const. art. VIII, §§ 1 and 3. However, the supreme court concluded that the district court was correct in holding that the analysis under art. VIII, §§ 1 and 3, and under the equal protection was the same for purposes of the taxpayer's case. In determining whether the vo-tech levy violated the state or federal constitutions, the district court properly held that the vo-tech levy was rationally related to the legitimate government interest of providing partial funding for the vo-techs because the vo-techs provided specific benefits to their individual counties as the attendance at the vo-techs was primarily by local residents. Therefore, the attendance by local residents and the course offerings served as a rational basis for putting the counties with vo-techs in a separate class for purposes of the vo-tech levy. Therefore, the vo-tech levy as mandated by Mont. Code Ann. § 20-25-439(1) did not violate the state or federal constitutions.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the district court's judgment.

Geil v. Missoula Irrigation Dist.


No. 01-364


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2002 MT 269; 312 Mont. 320; 59 P.3d 398; 2002 Mont. LEXIS 533


March 21, 2002, Submitted on Briefs  
November 26, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication December 12, 2002.
Related proceeding at RSG Holdings v. Missoula Irrigation Dist., 2004 MT 214, 2004 Mont. LEXIS 399 (Aug. 17, 2004)
Subsequent appeal at Geil-Hoeg v. Missoula Irrigation Dist., 2004 MT 217, 2004 Mont. LEXIS 389 (Aug. 17, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Fourth Judicial District, In and For the County of Missoula, Honorable John W. Larson, Judge Presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Missoula Irrigation District (MID) sought review of the decision of the District Court of the Fourth Judicial District, Missoula County (Montana), which found in favor of petitioners, taxpayers, in an action arguing that the taxpayers should not be required to pay a yearly tax for irrigation services.


OVERVIEW: The case involved petitions alleging taxation without irrigation. The taxpayers claimed that they could not obtain the irrigation water encompassed by the MID and they should not be required to pay a yearly tax for irrigation services. The MID appealed an adverse decision, and the court affirmed, stating that Mont. Code Ann. § 85-7-1802(2) (1997) did not deny equal protection under the law, as the legislative objective was legitimate. Whether the legislation applied to the entire state or only to one county, the declared objective was the same: to offer persons who were not served by the irrigation district works relief from assessments. In addition, the legislature's classification was rationally related to its objective, because its narrow classification was simply a valid legislative choice in an attempt to deal with a problem one step at a time. The court further stated that Mont. Code Ann. §§ 85-7-1802(2), 85-7-1846 (1997) did not deny due process of the law, because there was an adequate opportunity for a hearing. The MID failed to show that the four res judicata elements were met; accordingly that doctrine did not prohibit the exclusion of land from the MID.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the district court against the MID, in the taxpayers' action challenging a yearly tax for irrigation services.

Powder River County v. State

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, counties and individuals, instituted declaratory judgment actions challenging the validity of the local severance tax on net proceeds of oil and natural gas and a coal gross proceeds tax. The District Court of the 16th Judicial District, County of Rosebud, Montana, entered summary judgment for defendant state, defendant Montana Department of Revenue, and intervenor energy company. The counties and individuals appealed.


OVERVIEW: The counties and the individuals specifically challenged the legislature's decision to tax coal gross proceeds and oil and gas net proceeds in support of school equalization funding. At issue on appeal was whether the district court erred in concluding that classification of property for taxation was not statutorily or constitutionally mandated in Montana. Brimer v. State


No. 02-083


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


2002 MT 252N; 2002 Mont. LEXIS 520


June 27, 2002, Submitted on Briefs  
November 21, 2002, Decided


NOTICE:   [**1] PURSUANT TO THE APPLICABLE MONTANA CODE SECTION THIS OPINION IS NOT DESIGNATED FOR PUBLICATION.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Tenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Fergus, The Honorable E. Wayne Phillips, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was sentenced and convicted of obstructing a peace officer, partner or family member assault, and felony assault, and filed a petition for postconviction relief. The Tenth Judicial District Court for Fergus County, Montana, found defendant's petition for postconviction relief to be untimely and dismissed the petition. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The district court found that defendant's petition for postconviction relief was procedurally barred by the statute of limitations provided in Mont. Code Ann. § 46-21-102(1). Defendant argued that the district court erred by not addressing the merits of his petition, and alleged that the "interests of justice" required that counsel be appointed to assist him with his petition for postconviction relief. The State argued that pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 46-21-102(1), the district court properly dismissed defendant's petition on procedural grounds. The reviewing court ruled that defendant's petition was untimely, as his petition for postconviction relief was filed more than nine months after the statute of limitations had expired. Defendant's conviction became final on December 26, 1999, and he had until December 26, 2000, to file a timely petition for postconviction relief. Defendant's petition for postconviction relief was filed on October 4, 2001, more than nine months after the statute of limitations had expired. Furthermore, there was no basis upon which the reviewing court could conclude that a miscarriage of justice occurred in defendant's case.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the Thirteenth Judicial District, County of Yellowstone, Montana, denied defendants' motion to suppress, they separately pled guilty to arson and arson by accountability. They both received suspended sentences. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: The fire department was called to defendants' home when a neighbor observed flames consuming a portion of the structure. Code Ann. § 46-5-221.The appellate court held after evaluating the facts contained in the search warrant application and in light of the totality of the circumstances, the application set forth facts establishing a fair probability that the crime of arson had occurred at defendants' residence.

In re G.S.

SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA

DISPOSITION: District Court's judgment, granting temporary legal custody of G.S. and S.S. to DPHHS affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee State petitioned the Sixth Judicial District Court, Montana, for temporary legal custody of two children who were defined as Indian children under the Indian Child Welfare Act, 25 U.S.C.S. § 1901 et seq. The district court granted the Montana Department of Public Health and Human Services (DPHHS) temporary legal custody of the children for a period of 180 days. Appellant mother challenged the decision.


OVERVIEW: After a domestic violence incident between the parents, and allegations the father assaulted one of the children, the DPHHS removed the children from the custody of their natural mother. The children's natural father and the oldest child were enrolled members of a tribe, and the other child was eligible for enrollment. Therefore, the Indian Child Welfare Act was applicable. In applying a clear and convincing standard, the appellate court concluded the record showed evidence that active efforts were employed by DPHHS to provide remedial and rehabilitative services. The appellate court felt that it was unrealistic to require DPHHS to demonstrate compliance with 25 U.S.C.S. § 1912(d) prior to a show cause hearing on the underlying proceeding. The district court found that the father had an extensive criminal history, including several assaults on the mother. Clear and convincing evidence supported the district court's conclusion that the mother's continued custody of the children would likely result in serious emotional or physical damage to the children. The appellate court declined to address the mother's constitutional challenge.
